"summary": {"eng": "May 4 -- GRAND FORKS -- The share of North Dakota students filling out the federal application for college financial aid is falling, and it's falling fastest for lower-income students.\n\nThat's according to data compiled by the National College Attainment Network, which tracks the share of high school seniors completing the Free Application for Federal Student Aid at schools across the country and compares it against factors such as geographic locale and poverty level.\n\nIn North Dakota, the number"}, "location": {"type": "place", "label": {"eng": "Norwich, {"uri": "2024-05-345729836", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-05-05", "time": "15:46:03", "dateTime": "2024-05-05T15:46:03Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-05-05T15:30:00Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.95686274766922, "url": "https://dailyprogress.com/news/nation-world/education/college-financial-aid-applications-fafsa-enrollment-declines/article_4a3e1e8f-aef5-5913-b6e7-8b6a833f3064.html", "title": "Experts fear 'catastrophic' college declines thanks to botched FAFSA rollout", "body": "Students are facing an impossible decision. Tuition is expensive, and they will soon need to choose a college - potentially without knowing how they're going to pay for it due to delays in the rollout of the new FAFSA. CBS 2's Sabrina Franza reports.\n\nWASHINGTON -- The last thing standing between Ashnaelle Bijoux and her college dream is the FAFSA form -- a financial aid application that's supposed to help students go to college, but is blocking her instead. She has tried to submit it over and over. Every time, it fails to go through.\n\n\"I feel overwhelmed and stressed out,\" said Bijoux, 19. She came close to tears the last time she tried the form. \"I feel like I'm being held back.\"\n\nNormally a time of celebration for high school seniors, this spring has been marred by the federal government's botched rollout of the new FAFSA application. By May 1, students usually know where they're headed to college in the fall. This year, most still haven't received financial aid offers. Three months before the start of fall classes, many don't know where they're going to college, or how they're going to pay for it.\n\n\"We're asking them to make probably one of the biggest financial decisions -- and decisions that will have the biggest implications on their lives going forward -- without all of the information,\" said Justin Draeger, president and CEO of the National Association of Student Financial Aid Administrators.\n\nThe FAFSA, or Free Application for Federal Student Aid, went through a massive overhaul that was supposed to make it simpler and shorter. But a series of blunders by the Education Department made it harder than ever, delaying college decisions by months and raising fears that hundreds of thousands of students will forgo college entirely.\n\nAcross the United States, the number of students who have successfully submitted the FAFSA is down 29% from this time last year, and it's even worse at schools with more low-income students, according to the National College Attainment Network.\n\nThe group's CEO, Kim Cook, warned members of Congress this month about a potentially \"catastrophic\" drop in college enrollments that would make the decreases of the pandemic seem mild.\n\nFor Bijoux, of Norwich, Connecticut, the FAFSA problems threaten to undermine the promise of higher education.\n\nTo her, college is a chance to seize the opportunities that weren't available to her mother, who immigrated from Haiti to the U.S. as an adult. Bijoux hopes to become a therapist and set a positive example for her three younger brothers.\n\nIf her FAFSA goes through, she should be eligible for enough financial aid to help with the $ 13,000-a-year tuition at Southern Connecticut State University. If not, she might go to a local community college, but even that would require loans if she can't complete the FAFSA.\n\nEducation\n\nFAFSA WOES DELAY COLLEGE DECISION DAYS (3pmET)\n\n\"That's why it hurts, because it's like you work so hard to go somewhere and do something and make something of yourself,\" Bijoux said. \"I thought I would start at a four-year (college) and then work hard continuously, like I've been doing basically my whole life. But that's not the case.\"\n\nThe updated FAFSA form has one section filled out by students and another by their parents. But when Bijoux finishes her part, nothing shows up on her mother's online account. She keeps trying, but nothing seems to change.\n\nSimilar problems have been reported across the country, along with numerous other bugs that the Education Department has scrambled to fix. Families who call for customer service have faced long wait times or say the call center hung up on them.\n\nIt \"drains all the momentum\" from families working to send their children to college, especially those navigating the process for the first time, said Anne Zinn, a counselor at Norwich Free Academy, where Bijoux goes to school.\n\n\"I can only say so many times, 'Just be patient, just be patient,' before they throw their hands up and they're like, 'Why am I doing this? I'm just gonna go get a job,'\" she said.\n\nThe rollout has attracted bipartisan criticism in Congress, and it's being investigated at the request of Republicans. Richard Cordray, the federal student loan chief who oversaw the FAFSA update, has announced he will step down at the end of June.\n\nFor colleges, too, the delays pose a major threat.\n\nEnrollment decreases like those being projected now could put many small colleges out of business, or necessitate deep cuts in staff. Some colleges are pushing for emergency relief just to stay afloat, said Angel P\u00e9rez, CEO of the National Association for College Admission Counseling.\n\n\"If they don't get checks from the federal government to basically get them through next year, they will not survive,\" P\u00e9rez said.\n\nThe FAFSA has been the linchpin of student financial aid for decades. It's used to determine eligibility for the federal Pell grant, a scholarship for low-income students, and it's required to receive federal student loans. Colleges and states also use FAFSA to distribute their own scholarships.\n\nFAFSA had long been maligned for being tedious, difficult and intimidating to families without college experience. Congress passed legislation in 2020 meant to simplify the form. The Education Department was ordered to reduce the number of questions from more than 100 to about 40, and change the formula to expand aid to more students.\n\nProblems started piling up as soon as the new form went online in December, already months overdue.\n\nThe first applications were incorrectly processed using an outdated calculation for inflation. Later, a federal contractor miscalculated a different formula on more than 200,000 applications. Each mistake added to delays, leaving students waiting longer to hear anything about financial aid.\n\nEven more worrisome is a misstep that blocked students from finishing the form if they have a parent without a Social Security number. Advocates say the system locked out hundreds of thousands of students who are U.S. citizens or permanent residents but whose parents are not.\n\nFederal education officials say they're addressing lingering bugs and making progress. More than 8 million student applications have now been processed and sent to colleges, the agency said, and new applications are being processed within three days.\n\nSaving for your kids' college education? Everything you need to know about 529 plans\n\nSaving for your kids' college education? {"uri": "8112524208", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-05-05", "time": "15:45:03", "dateTime": "2024-05-05T15:45:03Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-05-05T15:44:43Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9529411792755127, "url": "https://omaha.com/news/nation-world/education/college-financial-aid-applications-fafsa-enrollment-declines/article_52f954a1-9593-5f1f-a2f7-587811fcc67a.html", "title": "Experts fear 'catastrophic' college declines thanks to botched FAFSA rollout", "body": "WASHINGTON -- The last thing standing between Ashnaelle Bijoux and her college dream is the FAFSA form -- a financial aid application that's supposed to help students go to college, but is blocking her instead. She has tried to submit it over and over. Every time, it fails to go through.\n\n\"I feel overwhelmed and stressed out,\" said Bijoux, 19. She came close to tears the last time she tried the form. \"I feel like I'm being held back.\"\n\nNormally a time of celebration for high school seniors, this spring has been marred by the federal government's botched rollout of the new FAFSA application. By May 1, students usually know where they're headed to college in the fall. This year, most still haven't received financial aid offers. Three months before the start of fall classes, many don't know where they're going to college, or how they're going to pay for it.\n\n\"We're asking them to make probably one of the biggest financial decisions -- and decisions that will have the biggest implications on their lives going forward -- without all of the information,\" said Justin Draeger, president and CEO of the National Association of Student Financial Aid Administrators.\n\nThe FAFSA, or Free Application for Federal Student Aid, went through a massive overhaul that was supposed to make it simpler and shorter. But a series of blunders by the Education Department made it harder than ever, delaying college decisions by months and raising fears that hundreds of thousands of students will forgo college entirely.\n\nAcross the United States, the number of students who have successfully submitted the FAFSA is down 29% from this time last year, and it's even worse at schools with more low-income students, according to the National College Attainment Network.\n\nThe group's CEO, Kim Cook, warned members of Congress this month about a potentially \"catastrophic\" drop in college enrollments that would make the decreases of the pandemic seem mild.\n\nFor Bijoux, of Norwich, Connecticut, the FAFSA problems threaten to undermine the promise of higher education.\n\nTo her, college is a chance to seize the opportunities that weren't available to her mother, who immigrated from Haiti to the U.S. as an adult. Bijoux hopes to become a therapist and set a positive example for her three younger brothers.\n\nIf her FAFSA goes through, she should be eligible for enough financial aid to help with the $ 13,000-a-year tuition at Southern Connecticut State University. If not, she might go to a local community college, but even that would require loans if she can't complete the FAFSA.\n\nEducation FAFSA WOES DELAY COLLEGE DECISION DAYS (3pmET)\n\n\"That's why it hurts, because it's like you work so hard to go somewhere and do something and make something of yourself,\" Bijoux said. \"I thought I would start at a four-year (college) and then work hard continuously, like I've been doing basically my whole life. But that's not the case.\"\n\nThe updated FAFSA form has one section filled out by students and another by their parents. But when Bijoux finishes her part, nothing shows up on her mother's online account. She keeps trying, but nothing seems to change.\n\nListen now and subscribe: Apple Podcasts | Google Podcasts | Spotify | RSS Feed | SoundStack | All Of Our Podcasts\n\nSimilar problems have been reported across the country, along with numerous other bugs that the Education Department has scrambled to fix. Families who call for customer service have faced long wait times or say the call center hung up on them.\n\nIt \"drains all the momentum\" from families working to send their children to college, especially those navigating the process for the first time, said Anne Zinn, a counselor at Norwich Free Academy, where Bijoux goes to school.\n\n\"I can only say so many times, 'Just be patient, just be patient,' before they throw their hands up and they're like, 'Why am I doing this? I'm just gonna go get a job,'\" she said.\n\nThe rollout has attracted bipartisan criticism in Congress, and it's being investigated at the request of Republicans. Richard Cordray, the federal student loan chief who oversaw the FAFSA update, has announced he will step down at the end of June.\n\nFor colleges, too, the delays pose a major threat.\n\nEnrollment decreases like those being projected now could put many small colleges out of business, or necessitate deep cuts in staff. Some colleges are pushing for emergency relief just to stay afloat, said Angel P\u00e9rez, CEO of the National Association for College Admission Counseling.\n\n\"If they don't get checks from the federal government to basically get them through next year, they will not survive,\" P\u00e9rez said.\n\nThe FAFSA has been the linchpin of student financial aid for decades. It's used to determine eligibility for the federal Pell grant, a scholarship for low-income students, and it's required to receive federal student loans. Colleges and states also use FAFSA to distribute their own scholarships.\n\nFAFSA had long been maligned for being tedious, difficult and intimidating to families without college experience. Congress passed legislation in 2020 meant to simplify the form. The Education Department was ordered to reduce the number of questions from more than 100 to about 40, and change the formula to expand aid to more students.\n\nProblems started piling up as soon as the new form went online in December, already months overdue.\n\nThe first applications were incorrectly processed using an outdated calculation for inflation. Later, a federal contractor miscalculated a different formula on more than 200,000 applications. Each mistake added to delays, leaving students waiting longer to hear anything about financial aid.\n\nEven more worrisome is a misstep that blocked students from finishing the form if they have a parent without a Social Security number. Advocates say the system locked out hundreds of thousands of students who are U.S. citizens or permanent residents but whose parents are not.\n\nFederal education officials say they're addressing lingering bugs and making progress. More than 8 million student applications have now been processed and sent to colleges, the agency said, and new applications are being processed within three days.\n\nSaving for your kids' college education? {"uri": "8110969150", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-05-04", "time": "10:40:08", "dateTime": "2024-05-04T10:40:08Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-05-04T10:37:58Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9215686321258545, "url": "https://wcfcourier.com/students-in-limbo/article_c3d05411-07b3-57ab-8d29-2aee28b5544b.html", "title": "STUDENTS IN LIMBO", "body": "Ashnaelle Bijoux has been unable to complete the FAFSA form due to a technical glitch.\n\nCOLLIN BINKLEY\n\nAssociated Press\n\nHIGHER EDUCATION\n\nWASHINGTON -- The last thing standing between Ashnaelle Bijoux and her college dream is the FAFSA form -- a financial aid application that's supposed to help students go to college, but is blocking her instead. She has tried to submit it over and over. Every time, it fails to go through.\n\n\"I feel overwhelmed and stressed out,\" said Bijoux, 19. She came close to tears the last time she tried the form. \"I feel like I'm being held back.\"\n\nNormally a time of celebration for high school seniors, this spring has been marred by the federal government's botched rollout of the new FAFSA application. By May 1, students usually know where they're headed to college in the fall. This year, most still haven't received financial aid off ers. Three months before the start of fall classes, many don't know where they're going to college, or how they're going to pay for it.\n\nPeople are also reading... Iowa homeowners age 65 and up are eligible for state tax exemption UPDATE WITH PHOTO: Police arrest naked man for blocking road in Waterloo Interchange to be constructed at Greenhill, Highway 58 intersection in Cedar Falls UPDATE: Motorcyclist injured in Wednesday Cedar Falls crash has died UPDATE: Second person charged in 2021 Traer homicide Summer hours, fees set at The Falls Aquatic Center in Cedar Falls Drake Relays: Nissen sisters relish chance to compete at same meet for first time, excel early in careers at UNI, Don Bosco Nebraska fugitive stopped on US Highway 20 near Grundy County line College Volleyball: Cedar Falls middle blocker commits to UNI College Softball: Hogan learned to 'Go for it' in dual-sport career at UNI NFL: Ben Sinnott says whole NFL draft experience has been surreal Robert \"Bob\" J. Brown Medo's takes the place of Restoran Bosna in Waterloo with grand opening Friday New Iowa law regulates traffic cameras, which generated $1.16 million for Waterloo in 2023 Authorities investigating death in Oelwein after body found in wooded area\n\n\"We're asking them to make probably one of the biggest financial decisions -- and decisions that will have the biggest implications on their lives going forward -- without all of the information,\" said Justin Draeger, president and CEO of the National Association of Student Financial Aid Administrators.\n\nThe FAFSA, or Free Application for Federal Student Aid, went through a massive overhaul that was supposed to make it simpler and shorter. But a series of blunders by the Education Department made it harder than ever, delaying college decisions by months and raising fears that hundreds of thousands of students will forgo college entirely.\n\nAcross the United States, the number of students who have successfully submitted the FAFSA is down 29% from this time last year, and it's even worse at schools with more low-income students, according to the National College Attainment Network.\n\nThe group's CEO, Kim Cook, recently warned members of Congress about a potentially \"catastrophic\" drop in college enrollments that would make the decreases of the pandemic seem mild.\n\nFor Bijoux, of Norwich, Connecticut, the FAFSA problems threaten to undermine the promise of higher education.\n\nTo her, college is a chance to seize the opportunities that weren't available to her mother, who immigrated from Haiti to the U.S. as an adult. Bijoux hopes to become a therapist and set a positive example for her three younger brothers.\n\nIf her FAFSA goes through, she should be eligible for enough financial aid to help with the $13,000-a-year tuition at Southern Connecticut State University. If not, she might go to a local community college, but even that would require loans if she can't complete the FAFSA.\n\n\"That's why it hurts, because it's like you work so hard to go somewhere and do something and make something of yourself,\" Bijoux said. \"I thought I would start at a four-year (college) and then work hard continuously, like I've been doing basically my whole life. But that's not the case.\"\n\nThe updated FAFSA form has one section filled out by students and another by their parents. But when Bijoux finishes her part, nothing shows up on her mom's online account. She keeps trying, but nothing seems to change.\n\nSimilar problems have been reported across the country, along with numerous other bugs that the Education Department has scrambled to fix. Families who call for customer service have faced long wait times or say the call center hung up on them.\n\nIt \"drains all the momentum\" from families working to send their children to college, especially those navigating the process for the first time, said Anne Zinn, a counselor at Norwich Free Academy, where Bijoux goes to school.\n\n\"I can only say so many times, 'Just be patient, just be patient,' before they throw their hands up and they're like, 'Why am I doing this? I'm just gonna go get a job,'\" she said.\n\nThe rollout has attracted bipartisan criticism in Congress, and it's being investigated at the request of Republicans. Last week, Richard Cordray, the federal student loan chief who oversaw the FAFSA update, announced he's stepping down at the end of June.\n\nFor colleges, too, the delays pose a major threat.\n\nEnrollment decreases like those being projected now could put many small colleges out of business, or necessitate deep cuts in staff. Some colleges are pushing for emergency relief just to stay afloat, said Angel P\u00e9rez, CEO of the National Association for College Admission Counseling.\n\n\"If they don't get checks from the federal government to basically get them through next year, they will not survive,\" P\u00e9rez said.\n\nThe FAFSA has been the linchpin of student financial aid for decades. It's used to determine eligibility for the federal Pell grant, a scholarship for low-income students, and it's required to receive federal student loans. Colleges and states also use FAFSA to distribute their own scholarships.\n\nFAFSA had long been maligned for being tedious, difficult and intimidating to families without college experience. Congress passed legislation in 2020 meant to simplify the form. The Education Department was ordered to reduce the number of questions from more than 100 to about 40, and change the formula to expand aid to more students.\n\nProblems started piling up as soon as the new form went online in December, already months overdue. The first applications were incorrectly processed using an outdated calculation for inflation. Later, a federal contractor miscalculated a different formula on more than 200,000 applications. Each mistake added to delays, leaving students waiting longer to hear anything about financial aid.\n\nEven more worrisome is a misstep that blocked students from finishing the form if they have a parent without a Social Security number. Advocates say the system locked out hundreds of thousands of students who are U.S. citizens or permanent residents but whose parents are not.\n\nThe Education Department on Tuesday said it's giving those parents a new way to enter their tax information manually. But as recently as this week, some students said they were still blocked from submitting the form.\n\nFederal education officials say they're addressing lingering bugs but making progress. More than 8 million student applications have now been processed and sent to colleges, the agency said, and new applications are being processed within three days.\n\nStill, the wait is far from over. It usually takes weeks for schools to prepare financial aid offers. Some colleges have extended decision deadlines to give students more time to weigh their options. But some stuck to May 1, forcing students to choose a college -- and make a non refundable payment to hold their spot -- without knowing all their scholarship options.\n\nIn West Virginia, Gov. Jim Justice on Tuesday declared a state of emergency that allows students to receive state scholarships without having their FAFSA processed by the federal government. West Virginia has the lowest number of college graduates in the nation, and the state's high school FAFSA completion rates are currently down nearly 40%, said Justice, a Republican.\n\n\"Really and truly, a lot of kids are sitting on the side lines ... wondering, 'Am I going to be able to go to college?' \" he said during a news briefing.\n\nIn Baltimore, Camryn Carter is waiting to find out if he'll get a full ride to the University of Maryland or face tens of thousands of dollars in student loans.\n\nA top student and captain of his baseball and wrestling teams, Carter sees college as a step up in life. He thinks back to the times in the grocery store line when he had to put items back on the shelf because his mom couldn't afford the bill. A college degree would give him the stability he didn't always have, the 18-year-old said.\n\nBut when he looks at tuition, it's intimidating. Along with Maryland, he's also considering McDaniel College, a private school in Maryland. If he enrolls there, he expects to borrow almost $30,000 a year.\n\n\"I try to make the best decisions now so I can have a good future,\" he said. \"I'm a little nervous that things won't work out. But I'm faithful.\"\n\n0 Comments Love 0 Funny 0 Wow 0 Sad 0 Angry 0\n\nBe the first to know\n\nGet local news delivered to your inbox!\n\nSign up! * I understand and agree that registration on or use {"uri": "8110759045", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-05-04", "time": "07:30:36", "dateTime": "2024-05-04T07:30:36Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-05-04T07:20:56Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9098039269447327, "url": "https://mcdowellnews.com/students-in-limbo/article_30ab7a84-943f-593e-b943-aee56c251084.html", "title": "STUDENTS IN LIMBO", "body": "Ashnaelle Bijoux has been unable to complete the FAFSA form due to a technical glitch.\n\nCOLLIN BINKLEY\n\nAssociated Press\n\nHIGHER EDUCATION\n\nWASHINGTON -- The last thing standing between Ashnaelle Bijoux and her college dream is the FAFSA form -- a financial aid application that's supposed to help students go to college, but is blocking her instead. She has tried to submit it over and over. Every time, it fails to go through.\n\n\"I feel overwhelmed and stressed out,\" said Bijoux, 19. She came close to tears the last time she tried the form. \"I feel like I'm being held back.\"\n\nNormally a time of celebration for high school seniors, this spring has been marred by the federal government's botched rollout of the new FAFSA application. By May 1, students usually know where they're headed to college in the fall. This year, most still haven't received financial aid offers. Three months before the start of fall classes, many don't know where they're going to college, or how they're going to pay for it.\n\nPeople are also reading... Former McDowell man serving life sentence for murder killed in prison McDowell shortstop Eli Elliott signs to play with Ferrum College City of Marion announces new city clerk McDowell family grateful for help during girl's medical crisis Man charged with driving vehicle of dead man A heckler tried to derail Trevor Noah's speech at Wake Forest University. He answered her question Man sentenced to prison for second-degree murder in Marion Alumni Spotlight: Ceara Carmack Victim, suspect in Monday shooting in Marion still hospitalized Leading the way: McDowell softball seniors come through one more time as championship nears Former McDowell High School counselor charged with sex acts on a student makes court appearance Couple charged by McDowell Sheriff's Office with theft Work begins to renovate McDowell County's Rec Center Appalachian State football player Jack Murphy dies Inmate charged with malicious conduct\n\n\"We're asking them to make probably one of the biggest financial decisions -- and decisions that will have the biggest implications on their lives going forward -- without all of the information,\" said Justin Draeger, president and CEO of the National Association of Student Financial Aid Administrators.\n\nThe FAFSA, or Free Application for Federal Student Aid, went through a massive overhaul that was supposed to make it simpler and shorter. But a series of blunders by the Education Department made it harder than ever, delaying college decisions by months and raising fears that hundreds of thousands of students will forgo college entirely.\n\nAcross the United States, the number of students who have successfully submitted the FAFSA is down 29% from this time last year, and it's even worse at schools with more low-income students, according to the National College Attainment Network.\n\nThe group's CEO, Kim Cook, recently warned members of Congress about a potentially \"catastrophic\" drop in college enrollments that would make the decreases of the pandemic seem mild.\n\nFor Bijoux, of Norwich, Connecticut, the FAFSA problems threaten to undermine the promise of higher education.\n\nTo her, college is a chance to seize the opportunities that weren't available to her mother, who immigrated from Haiti to the U.S. as an adult. Bijoux hopes to become a therapist and set a positive example for her three younger brothers.\n\nIf her FAFSA goes through, she should be eligible for enough financial aid to help with the $13,000-a-year tuition at Southern Connecticut State University. If not, she might go to a local community college, but even that would require loans if she can't complete the FAFSA.\n\n\"That's why it hurts, because it's like you work so hard to go somewhere and do something and make something of yourself,\" Bijoux said. \"I thought I would start at a four-year (college) and then work hard continuously, like I've been doing basically my whole life. But that's not the case.\"\n\nThe updated FAFSA form has one section filled out by students and another by their parents. But when Bijoux finishes her part, nothing shows up on her mom's online account. She keeps trying, but nothing seems to change.\n\nSimilar problems have been reported across the country, along with numerous other bugs that the Education Department has scrambled to fix. Families who call for customer service have faced long wait times or say the call center hung up on them.\n\nIt \"drains all the momentum\" from families working to send their children to college, especially those navigating the process for the first time, said Anne Zinn, a counselor at Norwich Free Academy, where Bijoux goes to school.\n\n\"I can only say so many times, 'Just be patient, just be patient,' before they throw their hands up and they're like, 'Why am I doing this? I'm just gonna go get a job,'\" she said.\n\nThe rollout has attracted bipartisan criticism in Congress, and it's being investigated at the request of Republicans. Last week, Richard Cordray, the federal student loan chief who oversaw the FAFSA update, announced he's stepping down at the end of June.\n\nFor colleges, too, the delays pose a major threat.\n\nEnrollment decreases like those being projected now could put many small colleges out of business, or necessitate deep cuts in staff. Some colleges are pushing for emergency relief just to stay afloat, said Angel P\u00e9rez, CEO of the National Association for College Admission Counseling.\n\n\"If they don't get checks from the federal government to basically get them through next year, they will not survive,\" P\u00e9rez said.\n\nThe FAFSA has been the linchpin of student financial aid for decades. It's used to determine eligibility for the federal Pell grant, a scholarship for low-income students, and it's required to receive federal student loans. Colleges and states also use FAFSA to distribute their own scholarships.\n\nFAFSA had long been maligned for being tedious, difficult and intimidating to families without college experience. Congress passed legislation in 2020 meant to simplify the form. The Education Department was ordered to reduce the number of questions from more than 100 to about 40, and change the formula to expand aid to more students.\n\nProblems started piling up as soon as the new form went online in December, already months overdue. The first applications were incorrectly processed using an outdated calculation for inflation. Later, a federal contractor miscalculated a different formula on more than 200,000 applications. Each mistake added to delays, leaving students waiting longer to hear anything about financial aid.\n\nEven more worrisome is a misstep that blocked students from finishing the form if they have a parent without a Social Security number. Advocates say the system locked out hundreds of thousands of students who are U.S. citizens or permanent residents but whose parents are not.\n\nThe Education Department on Tuesday said it's giving those parents a new way to enter their tax information manually. But as recently as this week, some students said they were still blocked from submitting the form.\n\nFederal education officials say they're addressing lingering bugs but making progress. More than 8 million student applications have now been processed and sent to colleges, the agency said, and new applications are being processed within three days.\n\nStill, the wait is far from over. It usually takes weeks for schools to prepare financial aid offers. Some colleges have extended decision deadlines to give students more time to weigh their options. But some stuck to May 1, forcing students to choose a college -- and make a nonrefundable payment to hold their spot -- without knowing all their scholarship options.\n\nIn West Virginia, Gov. Jim Justice on Tuesday declared a state of emergency that allows students to receive state scholarships without having their FAFSA processed by the federal government. West Virginia has the lowest number of college graduates in the nation, and the state's high school FAFSA completion rates are currently down nearly 40%, said Justice, a Republican.\n\n\"Really and truly, a lot of kids are sitting on the sidelines ... wondering, 'Am I going to be able to go to college?' \" he said during a news briefing.\n\nIn Baltimore, Camryn Carter is waiting to find out if he'll get a full ride to the University of Maryland or face tens of thousands of dollars in student loans.\n\nA top student and captain of his baseball and wrestling teams, Carter sees college as a step up in life. He thinks back to the times in the grocery store line when he had to put items back on the shelf because his mom couldn't afford the bill. A college degree would give him the stability he didn't always have, the 18-year-old said.\n\nBut when he looks at tuition, it's intimidating. Along with Maryland, he's also considering McDaniel College, a private school in Maryland. If he enrolls there, he expects to borrow almost $30,000 a year.\n\n\"I try to make the best decisions now so I can have a good future,\" he said. \"I'm a little nervous that things won't work out. But I'm faithful.\"\n\nTags Politics Education The Economy University Government Departments And Ministries Finance School Systems Job Market Medicine Trade Telephony Sports Be the first to Sarah Kragthorpe, owner of Jitters, on moving the coffee shop Sioux City man jailed after fatal South Sioux City shooting DEAR ABBY: Bitter son won't allow mom to find happiness Chix Chicken & Waffles, a popular Le Mars food truck, to open restaurant Woodbury County Court Report for April 30, 2024 Paul Gausman's attorney claims $53,000 in fees for lawsuit against Sioux City school board Meet the ushers who help Sioux City's Orpheum Theatre move Sioux City cultural inclusion liaison settles lawsuit with USD Blue Bunny, Jolly Time among Northwest Iowa products and companies vying for 'Coolest Thing Made in Iowa' Iowa homeowners age 65 and up are eligible for state tax exemption Funeral services set for Iowa trucker David Schultz Suspended Sioux City inclusion liaison accuses human rights director of harassment, defamation\n\n\"We're asking them to make probably one of the biggest financial decisions -- and decisions that will have the biggest implications on their lives going forward -- without all of the information,\" said Justin Draeger, president and CEO of the National Association of Student Financial Aid Administrators.\n\nThe FAFSA, or Free Application for Federal Student Aid, went through a massive overhaul that was supposed to make it simpler and shorter. But a series of blunders by the Education Department made it harder than ever, delaying college decisions by months and raising fears that hundreds of thousands of students will forgo college entirely.\n\nAcross the United States, the number of students who have successfully submitted the FAFSA is down 29% from this time last year, and it's even worse at schools with more low-income students, according to the National College Attainment Network.\n\nThe group's CEO, Kim Cook, recently warned members of Congress about a potentially \"catastrophic\" drop in college enrollments that would make the decreases of the pandemic seem mild.\n\nFor Bijoux, of Norwich, Connecticut, the FAFSA problems threaten to undermine the promise of higher education.\n\nTo her, college is a chance to seize the opportunities that weren't available to her mother, who immigrated from Haiti to the U.S. as an adult. Bijoux hopes to become a therapist and set a positive example for her three younger brothers.\n\nIf her FAFSA goes through, she should be eligible for enough financial aid to help with the $13,000-a-year tuition at Southern Connecticut State University. If not, she might go to a local community college, but even that would require loans if she can't complete the FAFSA.\n\n\"That's why it hurts, because it's like you work so hard to go somewhere and do something and make something of yourself,\" Bijoux said. \"I thought I would start at a four-year (college) and then work hard continuously, like I've been doing basically my whole life. But that's not the case.\"\n\nThe updated FAFSA form has one section filled out by students and another by their parents. But when Bijoux finishes her part, nothing shows up on her mom's online account. She keeps trying, but nothing seems to change.\n\nSimilar problems have been reported across the country, along with numerous other bugs that the Education Department has scrambled to fix. Families who call for customer service have faced long wait times or say the call center hung up on them.\n\nIt \"drains all the momentum\" from families working to send their children to college, especially those navigating the process for the first time, said Anne Zinn, a counselor at Norwich Free Academy, where Bijoux goes to school.\n\n\"I can only say so many times, 'Just be patient, just be patient,' before they throw their hands up and they're like, 'Why am I doing this? I'm just gonna go get a job,'\" she said.\n\nThe rollout has attracted bipartisan criticism in Congress, and it's being investigated at the request of Republicans. Last week, Richard Cordray, the federal student loan chief who oversaw the FAFSA update, announced he's stepping down at the end of June.\n\nFor colleges, too, the delays pose a major threat.\n\nEnrollment decreases like those being projected now could put many small colleges out of business, or necessitate deep cuts in staff. Some colleges are pushing for emergency relief just to stay afloat, said Angel P\u00e9rez, CEO of the National Association for College Admission Counseling.\n\n\"If they don't get checks from the federal government to basically get them through next year, they will not survive,\" P\u00e9rez said.\n\nThe FAFSA has been the linchpin of student financial aid for decades. It's used to determine eligibility for the federal Pell grant, a scholarship for low-income students, and it's required to receive federal student loans. Colleges and states also use FAFSA to distribute their own scholarships.\n\nFAFSA had long been maligned for being tedious, difficult and intimidating to families without college experience. Congress passed legislation in 2020 meant to simplify the form. The Education Department was ordered to reduce the number of questions from more than 100 to about 40, and change the formula to expand aid to more students.\n\nProblems started piling up as soon as the new form went online in December, already months overdue.\n\nThe first applications were incorrectly processed using an outdated calculation for inflation. Later, a federal contractor miscalculated a different formula on more than 200,000 applications. Each mistake added to delays, leaving students waiting longer to hear anything about financial aid.\n\nEven more worrisome is a misstep that blocked students from finishing the form if they have a parent without a Social Security number. Advocates say the system locked out hundreds of thousands of students who are U.S. citizens or permanent residents but whose parents are not.\n\nThe Education Department on Tuesday said it's giving those parents a new way to enter their tax information manually. But as recently as this week, some students said they were still blocked from submitting the form.\n\nFederal education officials say they're addressing lingering bugs but making progress. More than 8 million student applications have now been processed and sent to colleges, the agency said, and new applications are being processed within three days.\n\nStill, the wait is far from over. It usually takes weeks for schools to prepare financial aid offers. Some colleges have extended decision deadlines to give students more time to weigh their options. But some stuck to May 1, forcing students to choose a college -- and make a nonrefundable payment to hold their spot -- without knowing all their scholarship options.\n\nIn West Virginia, Gov. Jim Justice on Tuesday declared a state of emergency that allows students to receive state scholarships without having their FAFSA processed by the federal government. West Virginia has the lowest number of college graduates in the nation, and the state's high school FAFSA completion rates are currently down nearly 40%, said Justice, a Republican.\n\n\"Really and truly, a lot of kids are sitting on the sidelines ... wondering, 'Am I going to be able to go to college?' \" he said during a news briefing.\n\nIn Baltimore, Camryn Carter is waiting to find out if he'll get a full ride to the University of Maryland or face tens of thousands of dollars in student loans.\n\nA top student and captain of his baseball and wrestling teams, Carter sees college as a step up in life. He thinks back to the times in the grocery store line when he had to put items back on the shelf because his mom couldn't afford the bill. A college degree would give him the stability he didn't always have, the 18-year-old said.\n\nBut when he looks at tuition, it's intimidating. Along with Maryland, he's also considering McDaniel College, a private school in Maryland. If he enrolls there, he expects to borrow almost $30,000 a year.\n\n\"I try to make the best decisions now so I can have a good future,\" he said. \"I'm a little nervous that things won't work out. But I'm faithful.\"\n\n0 Comments Love 0 Funny 0 Wow 0 Sad 0 Angry 0\n\nBe the first to know\n\nGet local news delivered to your inbox!\n\nSign up! * I understand and agree that registration on or use {"uri": "8112526931", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-05-05", "time": "15:48:09", "dateTime": "2024-05-05T15:48:09Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-05-05T15:47:38Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9098039269447327, "url": "https://www.stltoday.com/news/nation-world/education/college-financial-aid-applications-fafsa-enrollment-declines/article_4abe0991-6505-5352-9a66-e82a2a36b544.html", "title": "Experts fear 'catastrophic' college declines thanks to botched FAFSA rollout", "body": "WASHINGTON -- The last thing standing between Ashnaelle Bijoux and her college dream is the FAFSA form -- a financial aid application that's supposed to help students go to college, but is blocking her instead. She has tried to submit it over and over. Every time, it fails to go through.\n\n\"I feel overwhelmed and stressed out,\" said Bijoux, 19. She came close to tears the last time she tried the form. \"I feel like I'm being held back.\"\n\nNormally a time of celebration for high school seniors, this spring has been marred by the federal government's botched rollout of the new FAFSA application. By May 1, students usually know where they're headed to college in the fall. This year, most still haven't received financial aid offers. Three months before the start of fall classes, many don't know where they're going to college, or how they're going to pay for it.\n\nWentzville superintendent's departure came after threats and police patrols Washington University faculty placed on leave, students 'houseless' after Gaza protest Cardinals games dropped by Comcast after Bally Sports deal falls through Busch Stadium needs renovations. Should St. Louis taxpayers kick in? Pro-Palestinian protests block traffic outside Washington University Southwest Airlines considering changes to its quirky boarding, pick-your-own seating practices Teen charged with breaking into more than 35 cars in St. Louis County Washington University students suspended after campus protest against war in Gaza St. Louis County man charged with 1966 Chicago killing, stabbing woman 125 times Video shows high-speed crash into Gus' Pretzel Shop. Owner hopes to reopen this week. Governor torpedoes appointment of former mayor in squabble with St. Louis senator Condemnation mounts for Washington University's 'appalling' response to protest Target to open this summer in Midtown St. Louis neighborhood BenFred: Here's why it just got harder to recruit against Eli Drinkwitz and Mizzou Hundreds gather at SLU to protest war in Gaza. 'It's my university's turn.'\n\n\"We're asking them to make probably one of the biggest financial decisions -- and decisions that will have the biggest implications on their lives going forward -- without all of the information,\" said Justin Draeger, president and CEO of the National Association of Student Financial Aid Administrators.\n\nThe FAFSA, or Free Application for Federal Student Aid, went through a massive overhaul that was supposed to make it simpler and shorter. But a series of blunders by the Education Department made it harder than ever, delaying college decisions by months and raising fears that hundreds of thousands of students will forgo college entirely.\n\nAcross the United States, the number of students who have successfully submitted the FAFSA is down 29% from this time last year, and it's even worse at schools with more low-income students, according to the National College Attainment Network.\n\nThe group's CEO, Kim Cook, warned members of Congress this month about a potentially \"catastrophic\" drop in college enrollments that would make the decreases of the pandemic seem mild.\n\nFor Bijoux, of Norwich, Connecticut, the FAFSA problems threaten to undermine the promise of higher education.\n\nTo her, college is a chance to seize the opportunities that weren't available to her mother, who immigrated from Haiti to the U.S. as an adult. Bijoux hopes to become a therapist and set a positive example for her three younger brothers.\n\nIf her FAFSA goes through, she should be eligible for enough financial aid to help with the $ 13,000-a-year tuition at Southern Connecticut State University. If not, she might go to a local community college, but even that would require loans if she can't complete the FAFSA.\n\n\"That's why it hurts, because it's like you work so hard to go somewhere and do something and make something of yourself,\" Bijoux said. \"I thought I would start at a four-year (college) and then work hard continuously, like I've been doing basically my whole life. But that's not the case.\"\n\nThe updated FAFSA form has one section filled out by students and another by their parents. But when Bijoux finishes her part, nothing shows up on her mother's online account. She keeps trying, but nothing seems to change.\n\nSimilar problems have been reported across the country, along with numerous other bugs that the Education Department has scrambled to fix. Families who call for customer service have faced long wait times or say the call center hung up on them.\n\nIt \"drains all the momentum\" from families working to send their children to college, especially those navigating the process for the first time, said Anne Zinn, a counselor at Norwich Free Academy, where Bijoux goes to school.\n\n\"I can only say so many times, 'Just be patient, just be patient,' before they throw their hands up and they're like, 'Why am I doing this? I'm just gonna go get a job,'\" she said.\n\nThe rollout has attracted bipartisan criticism in Congress, and it's being investigated at the request of Republicans. Richard Cordray, the federal student loan chief who oversaw the FAFSA update, has announced he will step down at the end of June.\n\nFor colleges, too, the delays pose a major threat.\n\nEnrollment decreases like those being projected now could put many small colleges out of business, or necessitate deep cuts in staff. Some colleges are pushing for emergency relief just to stay afloat, said Angel P\u00e9rez, CEO of the National Association for College Admission Counseling.\n\n\"If they don't get checks from the federal government to basically get them through next year, they will not survive,\" P\u00e9rez said.\n\nThe FAFSA has been the linchpin of student financial aid for decades. It's used to determine eligibility for the federal Pell grant, a scholarship for low-income students, and it's required to receive federal student loans. Colleges and states also use FAFSA to distribute their own scholarships.\n\nFAFSA had long been maligned for being tedious, difficult and intimidating to families without college experience. Congress passed legislation in 2020 meant to simplify the form. The Education Department was ordered to reduce the number of questions from more than 100 to about 40, and change the formula to expand aid to more students.\n\nProblems started piling up as soon as the new form went online in December, already months overdue.\n\nThe first applications were incorrectly processed using an outdated calculation for inflation. Later, a federal contractor miscalculated a different formula on more than 200,000 applications. Each mistake added to delays, leaving students waiting longer to hear anything about financial aid.\n\nEven more worrisome is a misstep that blocked students from finishing the form if they have a parent without a Social Security number. Advocates say the system locked out hundreds of thousands of students who are U.S. citizens or permanent residents but whose parents are not.\n\nFederal education officials say they're addressing lingering bugs and making progress. More than 8 million student applications have now been processed and sent to colleges, the agency said, and new applications are being processed within three days.\n\n0 Comments Tags Dcc Wire {"uri": "8112516547", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-05-05", "time": "15:37:14", "dateTime": "2024-05-05T15:37:14Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-05-05T15:36:48Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9019607901573181, "url": "https://pantagraph.com/news/nation-world/education/college-financial-aid-applications-fafsa-enrollment-declines/article_c5fcb7de-8c08-5bf1-8091-834c34920f49.html", "title": "Experts fear 'catastrophic' college declines thanks to botched FAFSA rollout", "body": "WASHINGTON -- The last thing standing between Ashnaelle Bijoux and her college dream is the FAFSA form -- a financial aid application that's supposed to help students go to college, but is blocking her instead. She has tried to submit it over and over. Every time, it fails to go through.\n\n\"I feel overwhelmed and stressed out,\" said Bijoux, 19. She came close to tears the last time she tried the form. \"I feel like I'm being held back.\"\n\nNormally a time of celebration for high school seniors, this spring has been marred by the federal government's botched rollout of the new FAFSA application. By May 1, students usually know where they're headed to college in the fall. This year, most still haven't received financial aid offers. Three months before the start of fall classes, many don't know where they're going to college, or how they're going to pay for it.\n\nPeople are also reading... Ex-Unit 5 teacher sentenced on reduced charges of trespassing in Decatur, assault in Nashville Carnival to return to Eastland Mall this week Dan Brady announces candidacy for Bloomington mayor Bloomington man gets 75-year prison sentence in 2023 murder at Victory Church Normal man arrested in home invasion, sexual assault investigation Normal police investigating report of home invasion, sexual assault Suspect in Heartland college carjacking granted pretrial release Flick Fact: The average age of a State Farm employee? 7 arrested on trespassing charges at ISU following week of protests WJBC mourns loss of former radio host Scott Laughlin Former Fairbury officer sentenced to 4 years in prison in sexual assault case Rivian receives $827M state incentive package to expand operations in Normal Flick Fact: The top public high school in Illinois outside of Chicago? PATH to lay off dozens in Bloomington after losing state 988 grant Man waiting murder trial accused of contacting witness from McLean County Jail\n\n\"We're asking them to make probably one of the biggest financial decisions -- and decisions that will have the biggest implications on their lives going forward -- without all of the information,\" said Justin Draeger, president and CEO of the National Association of Student Financial Aid Administrators.\n\nThe FAFSA, or Free Application for Federal Student Aid, went through a massive overhaul that was supposed to make it simpler and shorter. But a series of blunders by the Education Department made it harder than ever, delaying college decisions by months and raising fears that hundreds of thousands of students will forgo college entirely.\n\nAcross the United States, the number of students who have successfully submitted the FAFSA is down 29% from this time last year, and it's even worse at schools with more low-income students, according to the National College Attainment Network.\n\nThe group's CEO, Kim Cook, warned members of Congress this month about a potentially \"catastrophic\" drop in college enrollments that would make the decreases of the pandemic seem mild.\n\nFor Bijoux, of Norwich, Connecticut, the FAFSA problems threaten to undermine the promise of higher education.\n\nTo her, college is a chance to seize the opportunities that weren't available to her mother, who immigrated from Haiti to the U.S. as an adult. Bijoux hopes to become a therapist and set a positive example for her three younger brothers.\n\nIf her FAFSA goes through, she should be eligible for enough financial aid to help with the $ 13,000-a-year tuition at Southern Connecticut State University. If not, she might go to a local community college, but even that would require loans if she can't complete the FAFSA.\n\nEducation FAFSA WOES DELAY COLLEGE DECISION DAYS (3pmET)\n\n\"That's why it hurts, because it's like you work so hard to go somewhere and do something and make something of yourself,\" Bijoux said. \"I thought I would start at a four-year (college) and then work hard continuously, like I've been doing basically my whole life. But that's not the case.\"\n\nThe updated FAFSA form has one section filled out by students and another by their parents. But when Bijoux finishes her part, nothing shows up on her mother's online account. She keeps trying, but nothing seems to change.\n\nListen now and subscribe: Apple Podcasts | Google Podcasts | Spotify | RSS Feed | SoundStack | All Of Our Podcasts\n\nSimilar problems have been reported across the country, along with numerous other bugs that the Education Department has scrambled to fix. Families who call for customer service have faced long wait times or say the call center hung up on them.\n\nIt \"drains all the momentum\" from families working to send their children to college, especially those navigating the process for the first time, said Anne Zinn, a counselor at Norwich Free Academy, where Bijoux goes to school.\n\n\"I can only say so many times, 'Just be patient, just be patient,' before they throw their hands up and they're like, 'Why am I doing this? I'm just gonna go get a job,'\" she said.\n\nThe rollout has attracted bipartisan criticism in Congress, and it's being investigated at the request of Republicans. Richard Cordray, the federal student loan chief who oversaw the FAFSA update, has announced he will step down at the end of June.\n\nFor colleges, too, the delays pose a major threat.\n\nEnrollment decreases like those being projected now could put many small colleges out of business, or necessitate deep cuts in staff. Some colleges are pushing for emergency relief just to stay afloat, said Angel P\u00e9rez, CEO of the National Association for College Admission Counseling.\n\n\"If they don't get checks from the federal government to basically get them through next year, they will not survive,\" P\u00e9rez said.\n\nThe FAFSA has been the linchpin of student financial aid for decades. It's used to determine eligibility for the federal Pell grant, a scholarship for low-income students, and it's required to receive federal student loans. Colleges and states also use FAFSA to distribute their own scholarships.\n\nFAFSA had long been maligned for being tedious, difficult and intimidating to families without college experience. Congress passed legislation in 2020 meant to simplify the form. The Education Department was ordered to reduce the number of questions from more than 100 to about 40, and change the formula to expand aid to more students.\n\nProblems started piling up as soon as the new form went online in December, already months overdue.\n\nThe first applications were incorrectly processed using an outdated calculation for inflation. Later, a federal contractor miscalculated a different formula on more than 200,000 applications. Each mistake added to delays, leaving students waiting longer to hear anything about financial aid.\n\nEven more worrisome is a misstep that blocked students from finishing the form if they have a parent without a Social Security number. Advocates say the system locked out hundreds of thousands of students who are U.S. citizens or permanent residents but whose parents are not.\n\nFederal education officials say they're addressing lingering bugs and making progress. More than 8 million student applications have now been processed and sent to colleges, the agency said, and new applications are being processed within three days.\n\nSaving for your kids' college education? Families who call for customer service have faced long wait times or say the call center hung up on them.\n\nIt \"drains all the momentum\" from families working to send their children to college, especially those navigating the process for the first time, said Anne Zinn, a counselor at Norwich Free Academy, where Bijoux goes to school.\n\n\"I can only say so many times, 'Just be patient, just be patient,' before they throw their hands up and they're like, 'Why am I doing this? I'm just gonna go get a job,'\" she said.\n\nThe rollout has attracted bipartisan criticism in Congress, and it's being investigated at the request of Republicans. Richard Cordray, the federal student loan chief who oversaw the FAFSA update, has announced he will step down at the end of June.\n\nFor colleges, too, the delays pose a major threat.\n\nEnrollment decreases like those being projected now could put many small colleges out of business, or necessitate deep cuts in staff. Some colleges are pushing for emergency relief just to stay afloat, said Angel P\u00e9rez, CEO of the National Association for College Admission Counseling.\n\n\"If they don't get checks from the federal government to basically get them through next year, they will not survive,\" P\u00e9rez said.\n\nThe FAFSA has been the linchpin of student financial aid for decades. It's used to determine eligibility for the federal Pell grant, a scholarship for low-income students, and it's required to receive federal student loans. Colleges and states also use FAFSA to distribute their own scholarships.\n\nFAFSA had long been maligned for being tedious, difficult and intimidating to families without college experience. Congress passed legislation in 2020 meant to simplify the form. The Education Department was ordered to reduce the number of questions from more than 100 to about 40, and change the formula to expand aid to more students.\n\nProblems started piling up as soon as the new form went online in December, already months overdue.\n\nThe first applications were incorrectly processed using an outdated calculation for inflation. Later, a federal contractor miscalculated a different formula on more than 200,000 applications. Each mistake added to delays, leaving students waiting longer to hear anything about financial aid.\n\nEven more worrisome is a misstep that blocked students from finishing the form if they have a parent without a Social Security number. Advocates say the system locked out hundreds of thousands of students who are U.S. citizens or permanent residents but whose parents are not.\n\nFederal education officials say they're addressing lingering bugs and making progress. More than 8 million student applications have now been processed and sent to colleges, the agency said, and new applications are being processed within three days.\n\nSaving for your kids' college education? {"uri": "8111053335", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-05-04", "time": "12:01:34", "dateTime": "2024-05-04T12:01:34Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-05-04T11:57:57Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.8980392217636108, "url": "https://lacrossetribune.com/students-in-limbo/article_f68f1179-bdb6-545f-885a-a857e0251edb.html", "title": "STUDENTS IN LIMBO", "body": "Ashnaelle Bijoux has been unable to complete the FAFSA form due to a technical glitch.\n\nCOLLIN BINKLEY\n\nAssociated Press\n\nHIGHER EDUCATION\n\nWASHINGTON -- The last thing standing between Ashnaelle Bijoux and her college dream is the FAFSA form -- a financial aid application that's supposed to help students go to college, but is blocking her instead. She has tried to submit it over and over. Every time, it fails to go through.\n\n\"I feel overwhelmed and stressed out,\" said Bijoux, 19. She came close to tears the last time she tried the form. \"I feel like I'm being held back.\"\n\nNormally a time of celebration for high school seniors, this spring has been marred by the federal government's botched rollout of the new FAFSA application. By May 1, students usually know where they're headed to college in the fall. This year, most still haven't received financial aid offers. Three months before the start of fall classes, many don't know where they're going to college, or how they're going to pay for it.\n\nPeople are also reading... La Crosse orders City Brewery to stop the smells; daily fines begin Monday Steve Cahalan: Valley View Mall to add more tenants, 7 Brew Coffee drive-thrus coming to La Crosse Bellin Gundersen Health System files trademark application for Emplify Health name, logo Steve Cahalan: NOM Sips & Eats cafe to open in downtown La Crosse Amtrak adds second train between Twin Cities, Chicago; Borealis service will stop in La Crosse, Tomah, Winona La Crosse man arrested for possession of fentanyl during traffic stop Holmen residents face 18 criminal charges after major drug bust Toad's Cove ready to reopen as Centerville's only convenience store 1 year after devastating fire La Crosse area divorces Onalaska teen faces charges for uploading child pornography 'Slow, thoughtful process': Emplify Health to become new name of Gundersen, Bellin group Mount Horeb shooting: Students 'saw something' as teen approached school, notified staff La Crosse County home prices skyrocket 21% over last year; fewer sales than year prior Melrose man faces charges of stalking and vandalism in jealous rage Warrant issued for La Crosse man accused of reckless endangerment\n\n\"We're asking them to make probably one of the biggest financial decisions -- and decisions that will have the biggest implications on their lives going forward -- without all of the information,\" said Justin Draeger, president and CEO of the National Association of Student Financial Aid Administrators.\n\nThe FAFSA, or Free Application for Federal Student Aid, went through a massive overhaul that was supposed to make it simpler and shorter. But a series of blunders by the Education Department made it harder than ever, delaying college decisions by months and raising fears that hundreds of thousands of students will forgo college entirely.\n\nAcross the United States, the number of students who have successfully submitted the FAFSA is down 29% from this time last year, and it's even worse at schools with more low-income students, according to the National College Attainment Network.\n\nThe group's CEO, Kim Cook, recently warned members of Congress about a potentially \"catastrophic\" drop in college enrollments that would make the decreases of the pandemic seem mild.\n\nFor Bijoux, of Norwich, Connecticut, the FAFSA problems threaten to undermine the promise of higher education.\n\nTo her, college is a chance to seize the opportunities that weren't available to her mother, who immigrated from Haiti to the U.S. as an adult. Bijoux hopes to become a therapist and set a positive example for her three younger brothers.\n\nIf her FAFSA goes through, she should be eligible for enough financial aid to help with the $13,000-a-year tuition at Southern Connecticut State University. If not, she might go to a local community college, but even that would require loans if she can't complete the FAFSA.\n\n\"That's why it hurts, because it's like you work so hard to go somewhere and do something and make something of yourself,\" Bijoux said. \"I thought I would start at a four-year (college) and then work hard continuously, like I've been doing basically my whole life. But that's not the case.\"\n\nThe updated FAFSA form has one section filled out by students and another by their parents. But when Bijoux finishes her part, nothing shows up on her mom's online account. She keeps trying, but nothing seems to change.\n\nSimilar problems have been reported across the country, along with numerous other bugs that the Education Department has scrambled to fix. Families who call for customer service have faced long wait times or say the call center hung up on them.\n\nIt \"drains all the momentum\" from families working to send their children to college, especially those navigating the process for the first time, said Anne Zinn, a counselor at Norwich Free Academy, where Bijoux goes to school.\n\n\"I can only say so many times, 'Just be patient, just be patient,' before they throw their hands up and they're like, 'Why am I doing this? I'm just gonna go get a job,'\" she said.\n\nThe rollout has attracted bipartisan criticism in Congress, and it's being investigated at the request of Republicans. Last week, Richard Cordray, the federal student loan chief who oversaw the FAFSA update, announced he's stepping down at the end of June.\n\nFor colleges, too, the delays pose a major threat.\n\nEnrollment decreases like those being projected now could put many small colleges out of business, or necessitate deep cuts in staff. Some colleges are pushing for emergency relief just to stay afloat, said Angel P\u00e9rez, CEO of the National Association for College Admission Counseling.\n\n\"If they don't get checks from the federal government to basically get them through next year, they will not survive,\" P\u00e9rez said.\n\nThe FAFSA has been the linchpin of student financial aid for decades. It's used to determine eligibility for the federal Pell grant, a scholarship for low-income students, and it's required to receive federal student loans. Colleges and states also use FAFSA to distribute their own scholarships.\n\nFAFSA had long been maligned for being tedious, difficult and intimidating to families without college experience. Congress passed legislation in 2020 meant to simplify the form. The Education Department was ordered to reduce the number of questions from more than 100 to about 40, and change the formula to expand aid to more students.\n\nProblems started piling up as soon as the new form went online in December, already months overdue.\n\nThe first applications were incorrectly processed using an outdated calculation for inflation. Later, a federal contractor miscalculated a different formula on more than 200,000 applications. Each mistake added to delays, leaving students waiting longer to hear anything about financial aid.\n\nEven more worrisome is a misstep that blocked students from finishing the form if they have a parent without a Social Security number. Advocates say the system locked out hundreds of thousands of students who are U.S. citizens or permanent residents but whose parents are not.\n\nThe Education Department on Tuesday said it's giving those parents a new way to enter their tax information manually. But as recently as this week, some students said they were still blocked from submitting the form.\n\nFederal education officials say they're addressing lingering bugs but making progress. More than 8 million student applications have now been processed and sent to colleges, the agency said, and new applications are being processed within three days.\n\nStill, the wait is far from over. It usually takes weeks for schools to prepare financial aid offers. Some colleges have extended decision deadlines to give students more time to weigh their options. But some stuck to May 1, forcing students to choose a college -- and make a nonrefundable payment to hold their spot -- without knowing all their scholarship options.\n\nIn West Virginia, Gov. Jim Justice on Tuesday declared a state of emergency that allows students to receive state scholarships without having their FAFSA processed by the federal government. West Virginia has the lowest number of college graduates in the nation, and the state's high school FAFSA completion rates are currently down nearly 40%, said Justice, a Republican.\n\n\"Really and truly, a lot of kids are sitting on the sidelines ... wondering, 'Am I going to be able to go to college?' \" he said during a news briefing.\n\nIn Baltimore, Camryn Carter is waiting to find out if he'll get a full ride to the University of Maryland or face tens of thousands of dollars in student loans.\n\nA top student and captain of his baseball and wrestling teams, Carter sees college as a step up in life. He thinks back to the times in the grocery store line when he had to put items back on the shelf because his mom couldn't afford the bill. A college degree would give him the stability he didn't always have, the 18-year-old said.\n\nBut when he looks at tuition, it's intimidating. Along with Maryland, he's also considering McDaniel College, a private school in Maryland. If he enrolls there, he expects to borrow almost $30,000 a year.\n\n\"I try to make the best decisions now so I can have a good future,\" he said. \"I'm a little nervous that things won't work out. But I'm faithful.\"\n\nLove 0 Funny 0 Wow 0 Sad 0 Angry 0\n\nTags Politics Education The Economy University Government Departments And Ministries Finance School Systems Job Market Medicine Trade {"uri": "8112514619", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-05-05", "time": "15:35:52", "dateTime": "2024-05-05T15:35:52Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-05-05T15:35:16Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.8901960849761963, "url": "https://wcfcourier.com/news/nation-world/education/college-financial-aid-applications-fafsa-enrollment-declines/article_5399ac00-2ff4-5ee8-be43-01fa8e3981d6.html", "title": "Experts fear 'catastrophic' college declines thanks to botched FAFSA rollout", "body": "WASHINGTON -- The last thing standing between Ashnaelle Bijoux and her college dream is the FAFSA form -- a financial aid application that's supposed to help students go to college, but is blocking her instead. She has tried to submit it over and over. Every time, it fails to go through.\n\n\"I feel overwhelmed and stressed out,\" said Bijoux, 19. She came close to tears the last time she tried the form. \"I feel like I'm being held back.\"\n\nNormally a time of celebration for high school seniors, this spring has been marred by the federal government's botched rollout of the new FAFSA application. By May 1, students usually know where they're headed to college in the fall. This year, most still haven't received financial aid offers. Three months before the start of fall classes, many don't know where they're going to college, or how they're going to pay for it.\n\nPeople are also reading... UPDATE WITH PHOTO: Police arrest naked man for blocking road in Waterloo Iowa homeowners age 65 and up are eligible for state tax exemption UPDATE: Motorcyclist injured in Wednesday Cedar Falls crash has died UPDATE: Second person charged in 2021 Traer homicide Drake Relays: Nissen sisters relish chance to compete at same meet for first time, excel early in careers at UNI, Don Bosco Summer hours, fees set at The Falls Aquatic Center in Cedar Falls Interchange to be constructed at Greenhill, Highway 58 intersection in Cedar Falls Nebraska fugitive stopped on US Highway 20 near Grundy County line Medo's takes the place of Restoran Bosna in Waterloo with grand opening Friday College Volleyball: Cedar Falls middle blocker commits to UNI College Softball: Hogan learned to 'Go for it' in dual-sport career at UNI NFL: Ben Sinnott says whole NFL draft experience has been surreal College Men's Basketball: Coe College guard commits to UNI as grad transfer Robert \"Bob\" J. Brown New Iowa law regulates traffic cameras, which generated $1.16 million for Waterloo in 2023\n\n\"We're asking them to make probably one of the biggest financial decisions -- and decisions that will have the biggest implications on their lives going forward -- without all of the information,\" said Justin Draeger, president and CEO of the National Association of Student Financial Aid Administrators.\n\nThe FAFSA, or Free Application for Federal Student Aid, went through a massive overhaul that was supposed to make it simpler and shorter. But a series of blunders by the Education Department made it harder than ever, delaying college decisions by months and raising fears that hundreds of thousands of students will forgo college entirely.\n\nAcross the United States, the number of students who have successfully submitted the FAFSA is down 29% from this time last year, and it's even worse at schools with more low-income students, according to the National College Attainment Network.\n\nThe group's CEO, Kim Cook, warned members of Congress this month about a potentially \"catastrophic\" drop in college enrollments that would make the decreases of the pandemic seem mild.\n\nFor Bijoux, of Norwich, Connecticut, the FAFSA problems threaten to undermine the promise of higher education.\n\nTo her, college is a chance to seize the opportunities that weren't available to her mother, who immigrated from Haiti to the U.S. as an adult. Bijoux hopes to become a therapist and set a positive example for her three younger brothers.\n\nIf her FAFSA goes through, she should be eligible for enough financial aid to help with the $ 13,000-a-year tuition at Southern Connecticut State University. If not, she might go to a local community college, but even that would require loans if she can't complete the FAFSA.\n\nEducation FAFSA WOES DELAY COLLEGE DECISION DAYS (3pmET)\n\n\"That's why it hurts, because it's like you work so hard to go somewhere and do something and make something of yourself,\" Bijoux said. \"I thought I would start at a four-year (college) and then work hard continuously, like I've been doing basically my whole life. But that's not the case.\"\n\nThe updated FAFSA form has one section filled out by students and another by their parents. But when Bijoux finishes her part, nothing shows up on her mother's online account. She keeps trying, but nothing seems to change.\n\nListen now and subscribe: Apple Podcasts | Google Podcasts | Spotify | RSS Feed | SoundStack | All Of Our Podcasts\n\nSimilar problems have been reported across the country, along with numerous other bugs that the Education Department has scrambled to fix. Families who call for customer service have faced long wait times or say the call center hung up on them.\n\nIt \"drains all the momentum\" from families working to send their children to college, especially those navigating the process for the first time, said Anne Zinn, a counselor at Norwich Free Academy, where Bijoux goes to school.\n\n\"I can only say so many times, 'Just be patient, just be patient,' before they throw their hands up and they're like, 'Why am I doing this? I'm just gonna go get a job,'\" she said.\n\nThe rollout has attracted bipartisan criticism in Congress, and it's being investigated at the request of Republicans. Richard Cordray, the federal student loan chief who oversaw the FAFSA update, has announced he will step down at the end of June.\n\nFor colleges, too, the delays pose a major threat.\n\nEnrollment decreases like those being projected now could put many small colleges out of business, or necessitate deep cuts in staff. Some colleges are pushing for emergency relief just to stay afloat, said Angel P\u00e9rez, CEO of the National Association for College Admission Counseling.\n\n\"If they don't get checks from the federal government to basically get them through next year, they will not survive,\" P\u00e9rez said.\n\nThe FAFSA has been the linchpin of student financial aid for decades. It's used to determine eligibility for the federal Pell grant, a scholarship for low-income students, and it's required to receive federal student loans. Colleges and states also use FAFSA to distribute their own scholarships.\n\nFAFSA had long been maligned for being tedious, difficult and intimidating to families without college experience. Congress passed legislation in 2020 meant to simplify the form. The Education Department was ordered to reduce the number of questions from more than 100 to about 40, and change the formula to expand aid to more students.\n\nProblems started piling up as soon as the new form went online in December, already months overdue.\n\nThe first applications were incorrectly processed using an outdated calculation for inflation. Later, a federal contractor miscalculated a different formula on more than 200,000 applications. Each mistake added to delays, leaving students waiting longer to hear anything about financial aid.\n\nEven more worrisome is a misstep that blocked students from finishing the form if they have a parent without a Social Security number. Advocates say the system locked out hundreds of thousands of students who are U.S. citizens or permanent residents but whose parents are not.\n\nFederal education officials say they're addressing lingering bugs and making progress. More than 8 million student applications have now been processed and sent to colleges, the agency said, and new applications are being processed within three days.\n\nSaving for your kids' college education? "originalArticle": null, "storyUri": "eng-9538877", "eventUri": "eng-9538877", "location": {"type": "place", "label": {"eng": "Washington (state)"}, "country": {"type": "country", "label": {"eng":
